It stands as an invitation to evaluate every model of development on its ability to be inclusive and sustainable, to rebuild the relationship between economics and politics on the common good, and to acknowledge the critical and generative role of charity in public life.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                           | Charity and truth belong together                                                                                                                   | Lutherans would define saving truth Christologically and scripturally before socially. | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">65</mark> | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">Substantial agreement</mark> | Charity and truth belong together, though Lutherans would define saving truth Christologically and scripturally before socially.                                 |
| 42 | [Pope Francis’](https://www.vatican.va/content/francesco/en.html) social teaching develops along the lines of [_Gaudium et Spes_](https://www.vatican.va/archive/hist_councils/ii_vatican_council/documents/vat-ii_const_19651207_gaudium-et-spes_en.html), which invites us to view history through the lens of human hopes and vulnerabilities, and to bring them into dialogue with the Gospel. This approach emerges with particular clarity in [_Evangelii Gaudium_](https://www.vatican.va/content/francesco/en/apost_exhortations/documents/papa-francesco_esortazione-ap_20131124_evangelii-gaudium.html), where he states that the Christian proclamation has an intrinsic social dimension and calls for a Church capable of listening to the cry of the poor, migrants and victims of new forms of slavery. [Francis](https://www.vatican.va/content/francesco/en.html)’ insistence on a <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-limited-disagreement">synodal Church</mark>, a Church that “walks together,” that seeks to read the signs of the times in the light of the Gospel and <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-limited-disagreement">allows herself to be evangelized by the poor</mark> with whom she shares history, also fits into this perspective. <a id="_ftnref47" href="#_ftn47">[47]</a>                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                           | Care for the poor, migrants, and enslaved is a real Christian obligation                                                                            | The poor also need the preached Gospel, not only social inclusion.                     | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">80</mark> | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">Substantial agreement</mark> | Care for the poor, migrants, and enslaved is a real Christian obligation; the poor also need the preached Gospel, not only social inclusion.                     |
| 43 | In [_Laudato Si’_](https://www.vatican.va/content/francesco/en/encyclicals/documents/papa-francesco_20150524_enciclica-laudato-si.html), [Francis](https://www.vatican.va/content/francesco/en.html) provided the first significant systematic treatment of the environmental crisis in a social Encyclical, demonstrating that it is not an isolated issue, but rather the ecological aspect of the contemporary socio-economic crisis. His proposal for an integral ecology combined care for our common home with the preferential option for the poor, and strongly affirmed that “the cry of the earth and the cry of the poor” <a id="_ftnref48" href="#_ftn48">[48]</a> cannot be separated. In this light, the universal destination of goods was brought to the forefront, alongside the critique of a technocratic paradigm that seeks to reduce everything to an object to be dominated; the defense of human labor threatened by the mindset of waste; and the need for intergenerational justice. Finally, he advocated for genuine dialogue between those working in the fields of politics and finance, so that neither would become self-referential.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                        | The critique of the technocratic paradigm is one of the strongest themes in the document and fits a Lutheran warning against idolatry of technique. | No major disagreement noted.                                                           |   <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-strong">90</mark>    |      <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-strong">Strong agreement</mark>      | The critique of the technocratic paradigm is one of the strongest themes in the document and fits a Lutheran warning against idolatry of technique.              |
| 44 | Faced with the breakdown of the social fabric, a “world war being fought piecemeal,” individualistic globalization and the impact of the pandemic on community ties, [Francis](https://www.vatican.va/content/francesco/en.html), in [_Fratelli Tutti_](https://www.vatican.va/content/francesco/en/encyclicals/documents/papa-francesco_20201003_enciclica-fratelli-tutti.html) _,_ sought to revive the dream of a humanity that opts for social friendship and universal fraternity. He proposed a culture of encounter, a “better politics” capable of seeking the common good, paths of reconciliation and a world that ensures “land, housing and work for all.” <a id="_ftnref49" href="#_ftn49">[49]</a> Finally, in [_Dilexit Nos_](https://www.vatican.va/content/francesco/en/encyclicals/documents/20241024-enciclica-dilexit-nos.html), he showed that these significant social endeavors cannot be separated from a personal relationship with Christ. Turning to the word of God, he reminded us that the truest response to the love of the heart of Jesus is concrete love for our brothers and sisters, and affirmed that “there is no greater way for us to return love for love.” <a id="_ftnref50" href="#_ftn50">[50]</a>                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                              | Fraternity and concrete love are commendable                                                                                                        | Lutheran theology would root them in Christ’s mercy received by faith.                 | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">75</mark> | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">Substantial agreement</mark> | Fraternity and concrete love are commendable; Lutheran theology would root them in Christ’s mercy received by faith.                                             |
{: .table .table-striped .table-hover .commentary-matrix-widths .align-middle}

#### _Interpreting history in the light of faith_

| \# | Paragraph Text                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                            | Points of Agreement                                          | Points of Disagreement and Caution                                                           |                    Alignment Score                     |                              Score Interpretation                               | Rationale / Basis                                                                                                                                              |
|:--:|---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|--------------------------------------------------------------|----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|:------------------------------------------------------:|:-------------------------------------------------------------------------------:|----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|
| 45 | Considering this historical overview, it is clear that the Church’s Social Doctrine is not the result of a project devised at a desk, but rather the product of a patient process in which each pontiff — together with the [Second Vatican Council](https://www.vatican.va/archive/hist_councils/ii_vatican_council/index.htm) — made a unique contribution in light of the “new things” of each particular era. In response to the challenges of their time, each one interpreted historical changes according to the Gospel, bringing to light different aspects of a single heritage: the dignity of the person, the value of work, the universal destination of goods, solidarity and subsidiarity, care for creation and the centrality of peace and fraternity. The result is a harmonious, though not always linear, development that is marked by different emphases, progressive insights, and, at times, changes in perspective that do not break with what came before, but allow its implications to mature. If today we can speak of a _corpus_ of shared principles and criteria, it is because this faith-based interpretation of history has never been interrupted, remaining ever open to the challenges posed by each generation. It is to <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-limited-disagreement">the great principles of Social Doctrine, which direct the discernment of believers in their personal and public lives</mark>, that I now wish to turn our attention, in order to grasp more effectively their internal coherence and capacity to guide our times. | The concluding synthesis gives many useful social principles | Lutheran theology would treat them as moral reasoning under the Law, not as a second Gospel. | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-mixed">55</mark> | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-mixed">Mixed / qualified agreement</mark> | The concluding synthesis gives many useful social principles, but Lutheran theology would treat them as moral reasoning under the Law, not as a second Gospel. |
{: .table .table-striped .table-hover .commentary-matrix-widths .align-middle}

### CHAPTER TWO

#### FOUNDATIONS AND PRINCIPLES OF THE SOCIAL DOCTRINE OF THE CHURCH

| \# | Paragraph Text                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                          | Points of Agreement                                                                                                                            | Points of Disagreement and Caution |                       Alignment Score                        |                              Score Interpretation                               | Rationale / Basis                                                                                                                              |
|:--:|---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|------------------------------------|:------------------------------------------------------------:|:-------------------------------------------------------------------------------:|------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|
| 46 | The Social Doctrine of the Church is a living reality, in dialogue with history, cultures and sciences. At the same time, <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-limited-disagreement">it enshrines a core set of unchanging truths</mark>. For this reason, it can be considered a form of wisdom that is capable of guiding the personal and societal lives of believers even today. In this second chapter, I would like to focus on some of the foundations and principles of the Church’s Social Doctrine that will help us to interpret the “new things” of our time, particularly in view of the inherent dignity of the human person. In order to protect the human person in the age of artificial intelligence, I believe that today we must once again reflect on the common good, the universal destination of goods, subsidiarity, solidarity and social justice. I am convinced that a harmonious relationship between these principles requires that they be considered collectively, so that it becomes clear how they relate to and complement each other. | The list of dignity, common good, subsidiarity, solidarity, and justice offers helpful civic categories                                        | They require scriptural testing.   | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">65</mark> | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">Substantial agreement</mark> | The list of dignity, common good, subsidiarity, solidarity, and justice offers helpful civic categories, but they require scriptural testing.  |
| 47 | In offering these reflections, my hope is, first and foremost, to help the lay faithful and people of goodwill rediscover their duty of implementing the above-mentioned principles in their daily lives, family relationships, work and involvement in society. Thus, they will let themselves be inspired by the aim of embodying God’s love in the concrete events of life. At the same time, I would like to encourage academic institutions and universities to give fresh impetus to these principles, and to apply them in a way that will be relevant and effective in addressing the digital revolution. In this way, theological and philosophical enquiry will be able to further explore and support the Church’s pastoral journey, and contribute to <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-significant-disagreement">the Magisterium’s task of enlightening the consciences of the faithful</mark> and guiding their efforts to make the life of our societies more just and fraternal.                                                                           | Calling laypeople into public application is excellent; Luther’s doctrine of vocation strongly affirms lay responsibility in worldly callings. | No major disagreement noted.       |   <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-strong">95</mark>    |      <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-strong">Strong agreement</mark>      | Calling laypeople into public application is excellent; Luther’s doctrine of vocation strongly affirms lay responsibility in worldly callings. |
{: .table .table-striped .table-hover .commentary-matrix-widths .align-middle}

#### The foundations of Social Doctrine

##### _The human person: image of the Triune God_

| \# | Paragraph Text                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                             | Points of Agreement                                                                                                               | Points of Disagreement and Caution                                              |                       Alignment Score                        |                              Score Interpretation                               | Rationale / Basis                                                                                                                                         |
|:--:|----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|-----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|---------------------------------------------------------------------------------|:------------------------------------------------------------:|:-------------------------------------------------------------------------------:|-----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|
| 48 | The Church’s Social Doctrine brings us to the very heart of our faith: the mystery of the living God, revealed in Jesus Christ, who, as a communion of Persons — Father, Son and Holy Spirit — is love itself in relationship, expressed in the mutual gift of self and in sharing with the world. <a id="_ftnref51" href="#_ftn51">[51]</a> As the Council recalled, human persons are called to communion with God and “can fully discover their true selves only in sincere self-giving.” <a id="_ftnref52" href="#_ftn52">[52]</a> Indeed their deepest vocation is to <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-limited-disagreement">enter into the Trinitarian dynamic of love received and shared</mark>.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                 | Trinitarian communion as the ground of personhood is rich                                                                         | Lutherans would guard against moving too quickly from Trinity to social theory. | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">70</mark> | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">Substantial agreement</mark> | Trinitarian communion as the ground of personhood is rich, though Lutherans would guard against moving too quickly from Trinity to social theory.         |
| 49 | If the mystery of God as Love is the source of Social Doctrine, we see its most concrete expression in the face of Jesus Christ, the Incarnate Word. By becoming man, the Son of God enters our history and takes on human flesh, bringing with him the love that unites him to the Father and the Holy Spirit. In him, “the mystery of humanity truly becomes clear” <a id="_ftnref53" href="#_ftn53">[53]</a> because his humanity is completely free, open to others, capable of building healthy and beautiful relationships and committed to the total gift of self. Those who believe in him are engaged in the great work of renewal that began with the mystery of his passion, death and resurrection, and they <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-limited-disagreement">cooperate in building up the Kingdom of God</mark>, learning to embrace all men and women as brothers and sisters, children of one Father. In this way, both the proclamation of the Gospel and Christian life, guided by the action of the Holy Spirit, tend to bring about social consequences in the world. <a id="_ftnref54" href="#_ftn54">[54]</a> | Christ revealing man to himself is true if centered on incarnation, cross, and resurrection                                       | Social consequences follow but do not replace justification.                    | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">65</mark> | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">Substantial agreement</mark> | Christ revealing man to himself is true if centered on incarnation, cross, and resurrection; social consequences follow but do not replace justification. |
| 50 | At the heart of the Christian understanding of the human person lies the great biblical affirmation that men and women are created in the image and likeness (cf. _Gen_ 1:26-27) of the Triune God. Created for relationship, every human person is planned and willed by God to enter into communion with him, with others and with creation. Human dignity does not depend on a person’s abilities, wealth or position in life, nor on the right or wrong choices made; instead, it is a gift that precedes and transcends each person, endowed by God as an expression of his unfailing love. For this reason, the human person always remains the “way for the Church” <a id="_ftnref55" href="#_ftn55">[55]</a> and the heart of every authentic path of integral human development. <a id="_ftnref56" href="#_ftn56">[56]</a>                                                                                                                                                                                                                                        | The imago Dei basis for dignity is strongly Lutheran: human worth is received from God, not achieved by capacity or productivity. | No major disagreement noted.                                                    |   <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-strong">95</mark>    |      <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-strong">Strong agreement</mark>      | The imago Dei basis for dignity is strongly Lutheran: human worth is received from God, not achieved by capacity or productivity.                         |
{: .table .table-striped .table-hover .commentary-matrix-widths .align-middle}

##### _The equal dignity of all human beings_

| \# | Paragraph Text                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                             | Points of Agreement                                                                       | Points of Disagreement and Caution                                                                  |                       Alignment Score                        |                              Score Interpretation                               | Rationale / Basis                                                                                                                                                      |
|:--:|------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|-----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|:------------------------------------------------------------:|:-------------------------------------------------------------------------------:|------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|
| 51 | [Saint John Paul II](https://www.vatican.va/content/john-paul-ii/en.html) stated that, “this heightened sense of the dignity of the human person and of his or her uniqueness, and of the respect due to the journey of conscience, certainly represents one of the positive achievements of modern culture.” <a id="_ftnref57" href="#_ftn57">[57]</a> This statement follows the line already laid out by the [Second Vatican Council](https://www.vatican.va/archive/hist_councils/ii_vatican_council/index.htm), which had noted a growing recognition of the sublime dignity of all persons, their superiority over material things and their universal and inviolable rights and duties. <a id="_ftnref58" href="#_ftn58">[58]</a> It is important to ensure that this growth in appreciation of human dignity is not obscured by the pressure of new ideologies or very powerful interests in today’s world. Among these ideologies, I consider particularly insidious the one that suggests that every person must earn or justify his or her own worth, to the point of attributing greater value to those who are more efficient or effective. From this perspective, persons end up being reduced to a means of achieving results, a resource to be used and exploited, and are no longer recognized as a proper end in themselves who should never be instrumentalized. The value of persons, however, does not depend on what they achieve or produce. There are rights that apply to everyone simply by virtue of being human, and no human power can legitimately deny or arbitrarily limit them. <a id="_ftnref59" href="#_ftn59">[59]</a> | The critique of efficiency-based worth is excellent and especially relevant to AI ethics. | No major disagreement noted.                                                                        |   <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-strong">95</mark>    |      <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-strong">Strong agreement</mark>      | The critique of efficiency-based worth is excellent and especially relevant to AI ethics.                                                                              |
| 52 | When we speak of dignity, we do not always use the word in the same way. Sometimes we refer to moral dignity, namely the way in which a person directs his or her choices and actions. At other times, we think of social dignity, which refers to a person’s living conditions and the concrete respect received from society. In other cases, we refer to existential dignity, meaning the way in which a person perceives his or her own worth and the value of life. These aspects of dignity can be enhanced or diminished. In addition to these notions, there is also the more profound and important level of ontological dignity. This is the dignity that belongs to every human being simply by virtue of existing, of having been willed, created and loved by God. <a id="_ftnref60" href="#_ftn60">[60]</a> No sin, failure, humiliation or exclusion can diminish the profound value of a human life that God has willed and called into being. <a id="_ftnref61" href="#_ftn61">[61]</a>                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                   | The distinction among kinds of dignity can clarify civil argument                         | Lutherans would still emphasize that sinners retain creaturely dignity while needing justification. | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">65</mark> | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">Substantial agreement</mark> | The distinction among kinds of dignity can clarify civil argument; Lutherans would still emphasize that sinners retain creaturely dignity while needing justification. |
| 53 | The fundamental dignity of each person, therefore, is neither acquired nor earned, nor does it need to be justified. The recent Declaration [_Dignitas Infinita_](https://www.vatican.va/roman_curia/congregations/cfaith/documents/rc_ddf_doc_20240402_dignitas-infinita_en.html) offers a summary of the Church’s thinking on this subject: “<mark class="alignment-mark alignment-limited-disagreement">Every human person possesses an infinite dignity</mark>, inalienably grounded in his or her very being, which prevails in and beyond every circumstance, state, or situation the person may ever encounter” <a id="_ftnref62" href="#_ftn62">[62]</a> — in other words, always and without exception. <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-limited-disagreement">The dignity of every human being can be described as infinite</mark>, as [Saint John Paul II](https://www.vatican.va/content/john-paul-ii/en.html) stated, <a id="_ftnref63" href="#_ftn63">[63]</a> for two reasons: first, because the love of God, who calls us to friendship with him, is infinite; and second, his love is absolutely unconditional, in the sense that, even if we search endlessly, we will never find anything that can erase or deny it.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                    | Grounding dignity in God’s love is right                                                  | The word “infinite” should be used carefully, since only God is infinite by nature.                 | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">70</mark> | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">Substantial agreement</mark> | Grounding dignity in God’s love is right; the word “infinite” should be used carefully, since only God is infinite by nature.                                          |
{: .table .table-striped .table-hover .commentary-matrix-widths .align-middle}

##### _The supreme value of human rights_

| \# | Paragraph Text                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                   | Points of Agreement                                                                                   | Points of Disagreement and Caution                                                     |                       Alignment Score                        |                              Score Interpretation                               | Rationale / Basis                                                                                                                                  |
|:--:|------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|:------------------------------------------------------------:|:-------------------------------------------------------------------------------:|----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|
| 54 | The Church gratefully acknowledges that “the movement toward the identification and proclamation of human rights is one of the most significant attempts to respond effectively to the inescapable demands of human dignity.” <a id="_ftnref64" href="#_ftn64">[64]</a> In this regard, [Saint John Paul II](https://www.vatican.va/content/john-paul-ii/en.html) stated that the _Universal Declaration of Human Rights_, proclaimed by the United Nations on 10 December 1948, remains one of the highest expressions of the human conscience of our time. <a id="_ftnref65" href="#_ftn65">[65]</a> It is “a milestone on the long and difficult path of the human race.” <a id="_ftnref66" href="#_ftn66">[66]</a> For this reason, from the Christian perspective, human rights are not an external addition to the person, but an expression of intrinsic human dignity, which the international community is called to protect and promote.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                               | Human rights language can serve justice                                                               | It remains tethered to creation, natural law, and the protection of the neighbor.      | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">75</mark> | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">Substantial agreement</mark> | Human rights language can serve justice, provided it remains tethered to creation, natural law, and the protection of the neighbor.                |
| 55 | Human rights are inviolable, since they are “inherent in the human person and in human dignity.” <a id="_ftnref67" href="#_ftn67">[67]</a> Consequently, they are universal and inalienable. <a id="_ftnref68" href="#_ftn68">[68]</a> Precisely because they are grounded in the common dignity of every man and woman, they have practical consequences and legal effects, for “it would be vain to proclaim human rights if, at the same time, everything were not done to ensure the duty of respecting them, respect by all, in all places and for all.” <a id="_ftnref69" href="#_ftn69">[69]</a> Among these rights, the first is the right to life, from conception to its natural end, <a id="_ftnref70" href="#_ftn70">[70]</a> without which it is impossible to exercise any other right. When this fundamental right is denied — as in the cases of induced abortion, killing of the innocent and euthanasia — we are faced with choices that the Church considers gravely wrong. <a id="_ftnref71" href="#_ftn71">[71]</a>                                                                                                                                                                         | The defense of life from conception to natural death strongly aligns with Lutheran pro-life teaching. | No major disagreement noted.                                                           |   <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-strong">98</mark>    |      <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-strong">Strong agreement</mark>      | The defense of life from conception to natural death strongly aligns with Lutheran pro-life teaching.                                              |
| 56 | Looking at our own time, we cannot ignore the fact that the protection of human rights has been exposed to two particularly serious dangers. The first is that these rights are declared in a purely formal sense, while technological progress continues alongside covert or overt violations of human dignity. The second, which is in fact the root of the first, is the inability to recognize the foundation of their universality, since we have abandoned “the search for the solid foundations sustaining our decisions and our laws.” <a id="_ftnref72" href="#_ftn72">[72]</a> [Pope Francis](https://www.vatican.va/content/francesco/en.html) urged us not to underestimate this last issue. He pointed out that when reason seriously examines human nature, it is capable of discovering values that apply to everyone, since they derive from human nature. If this task of inquiry were abandoned, it is conceivable that rights considered untouchable today might, in the future, end up being questioned or denied by those in power, perhaps after having obtained only an apparent consensus from populations that are frightened or manipulated. <a id="_ftnref73" href="#_ftn73">[73]</a> | The warning that rights collapse without a foundation is important                                    | Lutherans would name that foundation as God’s created order and revealed Word.         |   <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-strong">85</mark>    |      <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-strong">Strong agreement</mark>      | The warning that rights collapse without a foundation is important; Lutherans would name that foundation as God’s created order and revealed Word. |
| 57 | Along with a greater awareness of the value of every human person and their rights, recognition of minority rights has also grown. Yet, there is still a long way to go to ensure that the rights of a great many, namely women, are equally and genuinely guaranteed throughout the world. It is a fact that “doubly poor are those women who endure situations of exclusion, mistreatment and violence, since they are frequently less able to defend their rights.” <a id="_ftnref74" href="#_ftn74">[74]</a> It is, therefore, not enough to state simply that men and women have equal dignity and rights; it is necessary that this be reflected in concrete decisions, such as in laws, access to employment, education, social and political responsibilities, and the way society listens to and values women’s contributions. As long as this gap persists, we cannot say that society truly and fully recognizes that women have the same dignity as men.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                             | Equal dignity of women is fully affirmable and grounded in creation and baptismal equality.           | Lutheran theology would still preserve ordered vocations where Scripture teaches them. |   <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-strong">85</mark>    |      <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-strong">Strong agreement</mark>      | Equal dignity of women is fully affirmable; Lutheran theology grounds it in creation and baptismal equality, while preserving ordered vocations.   |
| 58 | It is individuals that matter, each and every person, together with their families. Social movements, communal ideologies and grand political proclamations in favor of a population are worthless unless they lead to the flourishing of persons — men and women — with their inalienable rights. Similarly, it is not enough to extol individual freedom or private enterprise if we then allow a multitude of people to continue living without decent work, protections or access to basic necessities.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                      | The focus on concrete persons and families is strong                                                  | Abstract ideology must never replace neighbor-love.                                    |   <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-strong">90</mark>    |      <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-strong">Strong agreement</mark>      | The focus on concrete persons and families is strong; abstract ideology must never replace neighbor-love.                                          |
{: .table .table-striped .table-hover .commentary-matrix-widths .align-middle}

#### The principles of Social Doctrine

##### _The principle of the common good_

| \# | Paragraph Text                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                         | Points of Agreement                                                                     | Points of Disagreement and Caution                                                     |                       Alignment Score                        |                              Score Interpretation                               | Rationale / Basis                                                                                                                                           |
|:--:|------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|-----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|:------------------------------------------------------------:|:-------------------------------------------------------------------------------:|-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|
| 59 | Recognizing that every man and woman possesses an inalienable dignity, together with rights that no human power can betray or nullify, requires us to shape the way we live together, including our economic and political choices, and the makeup of our cities. From this arises the first major principle of Social Doctrine that I wish to highlight: the common good. We can describe it as the social expression of the dignity recognized in every person. When [Benedict XVI](https://www.vatican.va/content/benedict-xvi/en.html) referred to the non-negotiable values that the Church must always defend, he included among them “the promotion of the common good.” <a id="_ftnref75" href="#_ftn75">[75]</a> For a Christian, going beyond the narrow confines of one’s own interests and committing oneself, within the limits of one’s ability, to the common good is a non-negotiable value, as is the promotion of life.                                                                                                                                                                              | Linking the common good with non-negotiable human dignity is useful                     | Lutherans would stress that civil law protects outward life, not inward righteousness. | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">75</mark> | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">Substantial agreement</mark> | Linking the common good with non-negotiable human dignity is useful; Lutherans would stress that civil law protects outward life, not inward righteousness. |
| 60 | [The Second Vatican Council](https://www.vatican.va/archive/hist_councils/ii_vatican_council/index.htm) affirmed that the common good consists in “the sum total of social conditions which allow people, either as groups or as individuals, to reach their fulfillment more fully and more easily.” <a id="_ftnref76" href="#_ftn76">[76]</a> This definition provides us with a valuable initial reference point, because the common good cannot be reduced to a mere list of conditions or institutions. It is not the sum total of individual benefits, nor the intersection of their particular interests; it is a greater good that belongs to everyone, and it can only be achieved, nurtured and protected by our collective efforts. We can say that social action reaches its fullness when it is directed toward this shared good, just as a person’s moral action finds its fulfillment in the choice of the true good. <a id="_ftnref77" href="#_ftn77">[77]</a>                                                                                                                                         | The common good as social conditions for human flourishing is a sound civil definition. | No major disagreement noted.                                                           |   <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-strong">85</mark>    |      <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-strong">Strong agreement</mark>      | The common good as social conditions for human flourishing is a sound civil definition.                                                                     |
| 61 | In this sense, we can say that the whole is “greater than the sum of its parts” <a id="_ftnref78" href="#_ftn78">[78]</a> and that, for this very reason, “the mere sum of individual interests is not capable of generating a better world for the whole human family.” <a id="_ftnref79" href="#_ftn79">[79]</a> Indeed, it is an illusion to think that simply pursuing one’s own progress without caring for others is sufficient for contributing to the good of all. This view ignores the inherent and specific value of the common good, which is the result of an “interdependence” <a id="_ftnref80" href="#_ftn80">[80]</a> that creates a network of social good that expands and has an impact on people. The common good is a “plus,” the result of interaction and mutual influence that connects various actions, initiatives, efforts and decisions. If we were to add up the individual goods, we could not explain the existence of this “plus” that transcends them and, at the same time, enriches them.                                                                                          | The “whole greater than the sum” idea is useful socially                                | It should not swallow individual persons into collectivism.                            | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">70</mark> | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">Substantial agreement</mark> | The “whole greater than the sum” idea is useful socially, though it should not swallow individual persons into collectivism.                                |
| 62 | It is the pursuit of the common good that gives life to a people, understood not as a mere collection of individuals, but as a living reality in which people learn to recognize that they themselves are interconnected and jointly responsible for the _res publica_. In this sense, every person contributes to the building up of one’s people through “a slow and arduous effort calling for a desire for integration and a willingness to achieve this through the growth of a peaceful and multifaceted culture of encounter.” <a id="_ftnref81" href="#_ftn81">[81]</a> Working together for the common good means having a shared vision. It is clear that there are many ideological and practical differences among people, as well as differing interests and frequent disagreements, but that does not mean it is impossible to engage in dialogue to establish a set of basic agreements that enable the creation of a shared vision, upon which everyone can move forward together.                                                                                                                     | Dialogue for shared civic vision is wise                                                | Confessional truth, however, is not produced by dialogue.                              | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">65</mark> | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">Substantial agreement</mark> | Dialogue for shared civic vision is wise; confessional truth, however, is not produced by dialogue.                                                         |
| 63 | It is the State’s responsibility to ensure cohesion, unity and the proper organization of civil society, so that the common good can be pursued with everyone’s contribution. In practical terms, this means that public authorities have the delicate duty to “harmonize the different sectoral interests with the requirements of justice,” <a id="_ftnref82" href="#_ftn82">[82]</a> seeking a balance between individual interests and the common good, without leaving behind the most vulnerable. When politics abandons a long-term perspective and reduces itself to short-term calculations or sterile polarizations, then the language of the common good loses credibility, and, at the same time, social inequalities and divisions grow.                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                  | The State’s role in ordering civil society fits Lutheran doctrine of civil authority.   | No major disagreement noted.                                                           |   <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-strong">90</mark>    |      <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-strong">Strong agreement</mark>      | The State’s role in ordering civil society fits Lutheran doctrine of civil authority.                                                                       |
| 64 | This also applies to international politics. As the divide between nations widens, a mentality of confrontation and aggression begins to take hold, and the difficult path toward a more united and fraternal world suffers new and painful setbacks. In this context, speaking of a shared journey toward a more just development for the entire human family “sounds like madness.” <a id="_ftnref83" href="#_ftn83">[83]</a> Yet we must not lose hope. I invite everyone to conceive of ways of cooperating and of more effective <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-limited-disagreement">international institutions, capable of safeguarding the global common good</mark> without compromising the legitimate diversity of peoples and nations. Indeed, the promotion of the common good can never be separated from respect for the right of peoples to exist, to preserve their own identity and to contribute their unique qualities to the family of nations. <a id="_ftnref84" href="#_ftn84">[84]</a> Moreover, any attempt or plan to eliminate or subjugate a nation is gravely immoral and therefore unacceptable. | International cooperation can be good                                                   | Lutherans would beware utopian hopes in global institutions.                           |    <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-mixed">55</mark>    | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-mixed">Mixed / qualified agreement</mark> | International cooperation can be good, but Lutherans would beware utopian hopes in global institutions.                                                     |
{: .table .table-striped .table-hover .commentary-matrix-widths .align-middle}

##### _The principle of the universal destination of goods_

| \# | Paragraph Text                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                              | Points of Agreement                                                                                                                   | Points of Disagreement and Caution                             |                       Alignment Score                        |                              Score Interpretation                               | Rationale / Basis                                                                                                                                                   |
|:--:|-----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|----------------------------------------------------------------|:------------------------------------------------------------:|:-------------------------------------------------------------------------------:|---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|
| 65 | “Among the numerous implications of the common good, immediate significance is taken on by the principle of the universal destination of goods.” <a id="_ftnref85" href="#_ftn85">[85]</a> First of all, this principle reminds us that the earth’s goods — soil, water, air and natural resources — are given by God to the entire human family to sustain the lives of all, and that every person has an inherent right to the use of such goods, both now and in the future. [Saint John Paul II](https://www.vatican.va/content/john-paul-ii/en.html) recalled that, “God gave the earth to the whole human race for the sustenance of all its members, without excluding or favoring anyone.” <a id="_ftnref86" href="#_ftn86">[86]</a> Consequently, “it is not in accordance with God’s plan to use this gift in such a way that its benefits accrue solely to a select few.” <a id="_ftnref87" href="#_ftn87">[87]</a> Today, we are called to recognize that this universal destination applies not only to material goods, but also to immaterial and cultural goods.                                                                                             | The universal destination of goods expresses a biblical concern for stewardship and neighbor                                          | Its property implications require careful prudential judgment. | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">70</mark> | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">Substantial agreement</mark> | The universal destination of goods expresses a biblical concern for stewardship and neighbor, though its property implications require careful prudential judgment. |
| 66 | Certainly there is a right to private property, which has its own specific meaning and purpose, yet it is always subordinate to the universal destination of goods. According to [John Paul II](https://www.vatican.va/content/john-paul-ii/en.html), this subordination is the golden rule of social conduct and the “first principle of the whole ethical and social order.” <a id="_ftnref88" href="#_ftn88">[88]</a> In the Church’s tradition, property has been viewed as a means of protecting and managing goods so that they may better serve the common good. Since “the Christian tradition has never recognized the right to private property as absolute or inviolable,” <a id="_ftnref89" href="#_ftn89">[89]</a> its social function must not be considered a mere theological opinion, but a doctrine of the Church, already present in Sacred Scripture and in the writings of the Church Fathers. For this reason, [Pope Francis](https://www.vatican.va/content/francesco/en.html) reminded us that solidarity, when lived out in its fullest sense, also means “to restore to the poor what belongs to them.” <a id="_ftnref90" href="#_ftn90">[90]</a> | The social function of property can be affirmed under love and justice; Lutherans also strongly defend the commandment against theft. | No major disagreement noted.                                   | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">70</mark> | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">Substantial agreement</mark> | The social function of property can be affirmed under love and justice; Lutherans also strongly defend the commandment against theft.                               |
| 67 | Today, among the goods that are universally intended for everyone, we must also include new forms of property, such as patents, algorithms, digital platforms, technological infrastructure and data. In a context where the wealth of nations depends increasingly on knowledge and technology, when these goods remain concentrated in the hands of a few, without adequate forms of sharing and access, a new imbalance is created that contradicts the universal destination of goods. In turn, it widens the gap between the included and the excluded, between those who can participate in the digital revolution and those who remain on the margins. Furthermore, care for our common home and our responsibility toward the poor and future generations require that the use of the goods of creation and the new possibilities offered by technology be regulated in such a way as to respect the environment, avoid waste and prevent new forms of exploitation.                                                                                                                                                                                                | Applying property questions to data, patents, algorithms, and platforms is timely and morally serious.                                | No major disagreement noted.                                   | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">80</mark> | <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-substantial">Substantial agreement</mark> | Applying property questions to data, patents, algorithms, and platforms is timely and morally serious.                                                              |
{: .table .table-striped .table-hover .commentary-matrix-widths .align-middle}

##### _The principle of subsidiarity_

| \# | Paragraph Text                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                          | Points of Agreement                                                                                                       | Points of Disagreement and Caution             |                       Alignment Score                        |                              Score Interpretation                               | Rationale / Basis                                                                                                         |
|:--:|-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|------------------------------------------------|:------------------------------------------------------------:|:-------------------------------------------------------------------------------:|---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|
| 68 | The principle of subsidiarity stems from the very same understanding of the human person that has guided our reflection on dignity and the common good. If every woman and man is called to take ownership of his or her own life and to contribute to the formation of society, then social institutions must also respect and support this responsibility. The Social Doctrine of the Church refers to subsidiarity as the principle according to which the role of individuals, families, local communities and intermediary organizations should not be supplanted by higher-level authorities. Moreover, higher-level institutions must recognize, protect and promote the freedom and creativity of lower-level entities, coordinating their contributions so that they can cooperate effectively for the common good. <a id="_ftnref91" href="#_ftn91">[91]</a>                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                  | Subsidiarity aligns with a Lutheran respect for family, congregation, local authority, and vocation.                      | No major disagreement noted.                   |   <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-strong">90</mark>    |      <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-strong">Strong agreement</mark>      | Subsidiarity aligns with a Lutheran respect for family, congregation, local authority, and vocation.                      |
| 69 | Starting with [Leo XIII](https://www.vatican.va/content/leo-xiii/en.html) and the beginnings of modern social teaching, the Church has insisted that neither the individual nor the family should be subsumed by the State, but should be allowed to act freely, as far as possible, without harming the common good. <a id="_ftnref92" href="#_ftn92">[92]</a> [Saint John Paul II](https://www.vatican.va/content/john-paul-ii/en.html) took up and developed this perspective, noting that the political community is at the service of civil society and that the State must protect the common good, intervening when necessary, but without permanently supplanting the responsibilities of intermediary organizations and social institutions. <a id="_ftnref93" href="#_ftn93">[93]</a> Subsidiarity does not justify the State’s disengagement, but rather guides its actions. Indeed, public intervention is necessary precisely to enable all social actors to fulfill their mission without being stifled. It is the responsibility of the political community to create the conditions that allow individuals, families, associations and intermediary organizations to fulfil their mission in society, without being replaced or reduced to mere facilitators. <a id="_ftnref94" href="#_ftn94">[94]</a> | The warning against the State absorbing family and person is especially welcome.                                          | No major disagreement noted.                   |   <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-strong">90</mark>    |      <mark class="alignment-mark alignment-strong">Strong agreement</mark>      | The warning against the State absorbing family and person is especially welcome.                                          |
| 70 | This principle encourages us to move beyond any form of paternalistic or welfare-based management of societal life, but instead to promote a culture of shared responsibility in a State that values citizens’ initiative, and a civil society capable of forging bonds and mobilizing energies in the service of the common good. In accordance with the principle of subsidiarity, decisions are made at the closest level possible to the persons involved, thereby fostering community life and avoiding people being presented with decisions that have already been taken. In this way people can participate in the decision-making process.
